    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="1f5d1c04441e4729b4ef408fc6ef2017">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: 'dc830b5c-cd70-4547-bd69-f5e153d181d6'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d263571740" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Optimized sodium ion battery (SIB) carbon anodes with high stability supporting high power densities are a much-needed material class and therefore intensively researched. The optimum graphitization degree to accommodate sodium ions, while providing high conductivity, as well as the influence of particle size distribution or pore sizes on the performance of carbon anodes, is one of the most discussed topics in this field. While a lot of studies have been published discussing these questions, the convoluted nature of these parameters, originating from material synthesis constraints, usually prevents their independent optimization. Based on Mesoporous N-doped Carbon Nanospheres (MPNC) as model carbon material systems, the graphitization temperaturefor spherical particles with a monomodal particle size distribution (≈280&nbsp;nm) and a narrow pore size distribution (≈30&nbsp;nm) is optimized for faradaic sodium ion storage (plateau capacity) and electrodes with a very high power density of 2680&nbsp;W&nbsp;kg<sup>−1</sup> at 1000&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> and a remarkable capacity retention over 2000 cycles of 86 %, only losing 0.04 % of its specific capacity per cycle, are demonstrated.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202401188-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202401188-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Sodium ion batteries (SIB) are a promising solution for the increased demand in electrochemical energy storage systems. The demand arises from the global endeavor of achieving carbon neutrality, which critically relies on large-scale energy storage facilities for renewable energy sources like solar and wind, which are inherently intermittent in their energy generation.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202401188-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202401188-bib-0001_R_d263571606e434" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - J. Energy Storage">1</a>, <a href="#adfm202401188-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202401188-bib-0002_R_d263571606e434" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Energies">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>While SIB are listed in the ″Top Ten Emerging Technologies in Chemistry 2022″<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202401188-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202401188-bib-0003_R_d263571606e441" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Chem. Int.">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> by the IUPAC, their significance is further underscored by their ongoing commercialization, with industry leaders such as CATL and Faradion already actively distributing commercial SIB systems.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202401188-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202401188-bib-0004_R_d263571606e446" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4">4</a>, <a href="#adfm202401188-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202401188-bib-0005_R_d263571606e446" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - J. Mater. Chem. A">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In comparison to lithium ion batteries (LIB), SIB show decisive economic advantages like inherently low resource prices,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202401188-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202401188-bib-0006_R_d263571606e451" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Chem. Soc. Rev.">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> the overwhelming sodium resource abundance in the forms of Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> and Na<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub>, essentially no use of critical elements like cobalt and the possible replacement of copper by cheaper aluminum metal as the negative electrode current collector.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202401188-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202401188-bib-0007_R_d263571606e466" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Nat. Rev. Mater.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Due to these advantages, SIB appear to be an ideal candidate for large-scale energy storage systems, supporting the stability of the electricity grid through peak shaving, offering potential cost savings through price arbitrage,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202401188-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202401188-bib-0008_R_d263571606e471" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Energy Convers. Manage.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and storing and redistributing energy from intermittently producing renewable sources. These applications, however, demand for a highly rate capable battery system that is stable under high current densities and responsive to frequent power alterations within seconds or minutes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202401188-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202401188-bib-0009_R_d263571606e476" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Energy Environ. Sci.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Although highly rate-capable LIB are already successfully applied (e.g., in electric vehicles), fast-charging under high current densities commonly leads to a significant generation of heat, which needs to be carefully managed by internal thermal systems to avoid severe lithium plating and overheating.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202401188-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202401188-bib-0010_R_d263571606e482" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Innovation">10</a>, <a href="#adfm202401188-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202401188-bib-0011_R_d263571606e482" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Sci. Rep.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Commonly, layered oxides like LiCoO<sub>2</sub> are used as cathodes in LIB, which are prone to releasing oxygen at elevated temperatures. This poses a major safety hazard, as it potentially triggers an autocatalytic reaction with the electrolytes, leading to a thermal runaway.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202401188-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202401188-bib-0012_R_d263571606e489" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Ionics">12</a>, <a href="#adfm202401188-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202401188-bib-0013_R_d263571606e489" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Appl. Energy">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> For SIB, Prussian blue analogs and polyanion-based compounds have been successfully applied as inherently safer cathode materials instead.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202401188-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202401188-bib-0014_R_d263571606e495" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Batteries Supercaps">14</a>-<a href="#adfm202401188-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202401188-bib-0017_R_d263571606e495" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - J. Mater. Chem.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup>The facile adaptation from preexisting (LIB) infrastructure favors the rapid industrial adaptation of SIB required for the quick development of these large-scale storage systems and renders SIB a so-called “drop-in” technology.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202401188-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202401188-bib-0018_R_d263571606e500" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Batteries">18</a>, <a href="#adfm202401188-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202401188-bib-0019_R_d263571606e500" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Nanotechnol., Sci. Appl.">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> While fundamental processing techniques are expected to be directly transferable, the search for a suitable anode material proves to be challenging. While graphite is commonly used in LIBs, it has been shown to generate unfavorable interactions between sodium cations and the graphene layers of the graphite structure, leading to unsatisfactory capacities of below 35&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, when using standard carbonate electrolytes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202401188-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202401188-bib-0020_R_d263571606e507" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - J. Mater. Chem. A">20</a>-<a href="#adfm202401188-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202401188-bib-0023_R_d263571606e507" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Solid State Ionics">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Following the pioneering work of Dahn&nbsp;et&nbsp;al. in&nbsp;2000,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202401188-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202401188-bib-0024_R_d263571606e515" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> biomass-derived hard carbons have been intensely investigated as anode materials for SIB in the last 20&nbsp;years, owing to achieved specific capacities above 300&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g,<sup>−1[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202401188-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202401188-bib-0025_R_d263571606e520" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - J. Mater. Chem. A">25</a>-<a href="#adfm202401188-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202401188-bib-0029_R_d263571606e520" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Adv. Energy Mater.">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> as well as a low price and a favorable working voltage.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202401188-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202401188-bib-0025_R_d263571606e525" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - J. Mater. Chem. A">25</a>, <a href="#adfm202401188-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202401188-bib-0030_R_d263571606e525" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - ACS Nano">30</a>-<a href="#adfm202401188-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202401188-bib-0040_R_d263571606e525" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Carbon">40</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Although the structure of the hard carbons varies strongly with the chosen precursor, pyrolysis conditions, and optional activation procedures,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202401188-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202401188-bib-0041_R_d263571606e531" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Nanoscale">41</a>-<a href="#adfm202401188-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202401188-bib-0044_R_d263571606e531" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - Mater. Adv.">44</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> they generally possess randomly stacked graphene layers, micropores, and surface defect sites, which are all assumed to be taking part in sodium storage.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202401188-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202401188-bib-0020_R_d263571606e536" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - J. Mater. Chem. A">20</a>, <a href="#adfm202401188-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202401188-bib-0045_R_d263571606e536" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - Nano Lett.">45</a>-<a href="#adfm202401188-bib-0053" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202401188-bib-0053_R_d263571606e536" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 53 - Carbon">53</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Despite intense research efforts, no clear and agreed-upon mechanism for the sodium storage in hard carbons has been found so far.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202401188-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202401188-bib-0037_R_d263571606e542" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Molecules">37</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The discussion is based upon the voltage profile during galvanostatic cycling, which is commonly separated into a sloping region (&gt;100&nbsp;mV&nbsp;vs&nbsp;Na/Na<sup>+</sup>) and a plateau region (&lt;100&nbsp;mV&nbsp;vs&nbsp;Na/Na<sup>+</sup>)<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202401188-bib-0052" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202401188-bib-0052_R_d263571606e551" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 52 - Am. Chem. Soc.">52</a>, <a href="#adfm202401188-bib-0054" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202401188-bib-0054_R_d263571606e551" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 54 - Nano Energy">54</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> (see Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202401188-fig-0006">6</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information). Based on the current understanding, however, it can generally be assumed that the sloping region is mainly associated with the adsorption of sodium ions on the carbon surface and defect sites, while the plateau region is commonly associated with slower, but higher energy storage processes like intercalation between graphitic layers or micropore filling.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202401188-bib-0055" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202401188-bib-0055_R_d263571606e563" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 55 - Chem. Eng. J.">55</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Yet, more work is needed to achieve a fundamental mechanistic understanding, which will aid in improving performance factors like the initial coulombic efficiency (ICE) and rate capability, which play crucial roles in the facilitation of a large-scale application of SIB.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202401188-bib-0056" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202401188-bib-0056_R_d263571606e568" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 56 - Molecules">56</a>-<a href="#adfm202401188-bib-0058" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202401188-bib-0058_R_d263571606e568" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 58 - Chem. Eng. J.">58</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Cao and Liu et&nbsp;al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202401188-bib-0059" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202401188-bib-0059_R_d263571606e575" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 59 - J. Mater. Chem. A">59</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> for example, prepared a mesoporous carbon material from mesophase pitch using nano-CaCO<sub>3</sub> as the template, resulting in a hard carbon with a high specific capacity of 331&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 30&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. However, at a current density of 1000&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, the capacity retention was only 32 % (105&nbsp;mAh g<sup>−1</sup>). Bai&nbsp;et&nbsp;al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202401188-bib-0060" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202401188-bib-0060_R_d263571606e591" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 60 - Energy Storage Mater.">60</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> improved the electrochemical performance of a hard carbon anode by pre-engineering a solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) and achieved a specific capacity of 270&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 20&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, while achieving a capacity retention of 71 % (193&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>) at 1000&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> and 24 % (65 mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>) at 5000&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>.</p>
         
         <p>In the pursuit of improving the rate capability, three approaches are commonly chosen in literature: 1) Nanostructuring the carbon matrix and introducing porosity to ensure short sodium diffusion lengths and a large electrode/electrolyte interface;<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202401188-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202401188-bib-0026_R_d263571606e613" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Adv. Energy Mater.">26</a>, <a href="#adfm202401188-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202401188-bib-0029_R_d263571606e613" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Adv. Energy Mater.">29</a>, <a href="#adfm202401188-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202401188-bib-0045_R_d263571606e613" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - Nano Lett.">45</a>, <a href="#adfm202401188-bib-0061" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202401188-bib-0061_R_d263571606e613" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 61 - Nano Res.">61</a>-<a href="#adfm202401188-bib-0066" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202401188-bib-0066_R_d263571606e613" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 66 - Small">66</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> 2) utilizing spherical particles for a high packing density, isotropic charge transport and low tortuosity for the facilitation of fast ion diffusion in the interparticular space between the spheroids;<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202401188-bib-0067" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202401188-bib-0067_R_d263571606e618" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 67 - J. Mater. Chem. A">67</a>-<a href="#adfm202401188-bib-0070" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202401188-bib-0070_R_d263571606e618" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 70 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">70</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and&nbsp;3)&nbsp;nitrogen doping of the carbon material with the goal of modulating conductivity and defect concentration.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202401188-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202401188-bib-0025_R_d263571606e623" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - J. Mater. Chem. A">25</a>, <a href="#adfm202401188-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202401188-bib-0029_R_d263571606e623" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Adv. Energy Mater.">29</a>, <a href="#adfm202401188-bib-0071" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202401188-bib-0071_R_d263571606e623" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 71 - Phys. Status Solidi">71</a>-<a href="#adfm202401188-bib-0073" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202401188-bib-0073_R_d263571606e623" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 73 - Electrochim. Acta">73</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>By applying these strategies, several groups have achieved promising results regarding the rate capability. In 2023 for example, Tong&nbsp;et&nbsp;al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202401188-bib-0063" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202401188-bib-0063_R_d263571606e631" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 63 - Chin. Chem. Lett.">63</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> published their study about a multi-porous carbon material prepared by using SiO<sub>2</sub> nanospheres as the template, gelatin as the nitrogen source, and polytetrafluoroethylene as the carbon precursor. While achieving a specific capacity of 264&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 100&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, a retention in capacity of 85 % (224&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>) and 78 % (208&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>) was achieved when the current density was increased to 1000 and 5000&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. Furthermore, Jin&nbsp;et&nbsp;al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202401188-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202401188-bib-0027_R_d263571606e649" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> prepared nitrogen and boron co-doped carbon nanosheets using gelatin as the carbon and nitrogen precursor as well, while utilizing boric acid as the template. Their anode achieved a high specific capacity of 340&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 50&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> and retained 64 % (217&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>) at 1000&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> and 58 % (196 mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>) at 5000&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>.</p>
         
         <p>Despite these promising results, the overall energy storage mechanism of both materials is found to be dominated by capacitive contributions, as concluded from the results of scan-rate-dependent cyclic voltammetry, which will inevitably lead to a high self-discharge rate. This would be detrimental to large-scale energy storage systems supporting the electrical grid. A capacitive energy storage mode is also represented in the voltage profile of desodiation, which in both studies appears almost linear, in contrast to a plateau and a sloping region commonly observed for classical hard carbon anodes (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information). Further, unlike an anode with a voltage plateau close to the Na/Na<sup>+</sup> redox potential, utilizing anodes that predominantly store energy capacitively can significantly impair both energy and power density in a full cell, because the energy density of a full cell is obtained by integrating the voltage difference between an anode and a cathode with respect to the specific capacity, while the power density of a full cell is the product of the average charge/discharge voltage and the applied current density.</p>
         
         <p>In the pursuit of finding a suitable anode material for a highly rate capable battery, enabling longer-term grid storage applications, we herein present the use of mesoporous N-doped carbon nanospheres (MPNC) as an anode material for tailored electrodes in SIB. The MPNCs are synthesized as reported by us previously,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202401188-bib-0074" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202401188-bib-0074_R_d263571606e678" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 74 - Carbon">74</a>-<a href="#adfm202401188-bib-0079" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202401188-bib-0079_R_d263571606e678" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 79 - Sol. RRL">79</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> that is, via a hard templating approach based on the self-assembly of SiO<sub>2</sub> template nanospheres (23&nbsp;nm), and polyaniline species during the polymerization of aniline, serving both as nitrogen and carbon precursors. Their unique microstructure, surface properties and excellent electrochemical stability already contribute to the successful application of MPNC particles in various electrochemical fields, ranging from Pt-based oxygen reduction<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202401188-bib-0074" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202401188-bib-0074_R_d263571606e685" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 74 - Carbon">74</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and hydrogen evolution catalysts<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202401188-bib-0077" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202401188-bib-0077_R_d263571606e691" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 77 - Small">77</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> to (photo)supercapacitors.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202401188-bib-0075" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202401188-bib-0075_R_d263571606e696" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 75 - Sol. RRL">75</a>, <a href="#adfm202401188-bib-0078" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202401188-bib-0078_R_d263571606e696" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 78 - Adv. Mater. Technol.">78</a>, <a href="#adfm202401188-bib-0079" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202401188-bib-0079_R_d263571606e696" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 79 - Sol. RRL">79</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Herein we will utilize the model system character of the MPNC material, enabling the investigation of the ideal graphitization temperature, while keeping the particle size and pore size distribution mostly constant (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202401188-fig-0001">1</a>). This allows us to deconvolute the ideal microstructure for sodium ion storage in a faradaic way, investigated by XRD (<i>d</i>-spacing), Raman spectroscopy (graphitization degree and defect density) and HR-TEM investigation of these MPNC materials and derived electrodes, from morphological factors like pore size, particle size distribution, and particle packing within the electrode. Higher graphitization temperatures are beneficial due to the expected increase in electrical conductivity. But they also lead to a decrease in the interlayer <i>d</i>-spacing between graphitic planes, potentially leading to the same thermodynamically unfavorable interactions as observed in graphitic carbon. Furthermore, the amount of surface functional groups and heteroatoms is expected to decrease with increasing graphitization, lowering the reaction sites where irreversible reactions might occur, thus increasing the ICE. In contrast, this also reduces the density of adsorption sites for (pseudo)capacitive energy storage, potentially lowering the overall energy and especially power density. Overall, optimizing for the right graphitization temperature is a delicate balance, and further investigations are hence needed. Based on our MPNC model system with monodisperse particle sizes and tailored pore sizes, we could optimize the graphitization of the carbon microstructure while keeping morphological performance parameters constant. We thereby demonstrate a predominantly faradaic energy storage, with high power density and remarkable capacity retention for at least 2000 cycles, suitable for electrical grid energy storage.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202401188-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/2d5dd13c-40bf-4aa6-b279-057232dbc7a6/adfm202401188-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/2d5dd13c-40bf-4aa6-b279-057232dbc7a6/adfm202401188-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/47c4d0b7-5ba3-445d-9aea-1b8b5344e5bb/adfm202401188-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/2d5dd13c-40bf-4aa6-b279-057232dbc7a6/adfm202401188-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">

      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202401188-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202401188-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202401188-fig-0001">1</a> shows the workflow and scope of the presented approach, from material preparation to electrochemical characterization. First, the N-doped carbon nanospheres were prepared following a synthetic route reported by us previously, applying a silica-based hard templating approach, using 23&nbsp;nm SiO<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles (<b>MPNC23</b>) and aniline as a polymerizable carbon precursor.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202401188-bib-0070" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202401188-bib-0070_R_d263571606e746" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 70 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">70</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Second, we graphitized three comparable MPNC batches (A&nbsp;–&nbsp;C) at five different temperatures under inert atmosphere and made an in-depth physicochemical characterization of their properties (Batch A: 1000, 1300, and 1700&nbsp;°C, Batch B: 1500&nbsp;°C, and Batch C: 2000&nbsp;°C, the sample names always include one of these five temperatures to clearly identify the associated material). This includes a study of their micro structure, that is, their elemental composition, porosity/surface area and graphitization. Third, we prepared electrodes from these materials and assembled electrode T-cells with a sodium counter, as well as reference electrode. With these, we investigated the graphitization temperature-dependent sodium storage capability and efficiency. We deconvoluted the capacitive (surface controlled) and faradaic (diffusion controlled) contributions of the MPNC23 samples during galvanostatic charging (sodiation) and discharging (desodiation) and compared our results to other studies on SIB anode materials. Further, we investigated the charge storage performance of the best-performing MPNC23 sample of this study (graphitized at 1500&nbsp;°C) by cyclic voltammetry, its overall rate capability between 50 and 10&nbsp;000&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> as well as its long-term cycling capacity retention, and compared it to various carbon anodes from literature, demonstrating an outstanding performance of our MPNC23-1500 material.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202401188-sec-0030">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202401188-sec-0030-title">2.1 Material Characterization</h3>
            
            <p><b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202401188-fig-0002">2</a> demonstrates the highly uniform and reproducible MPNC23 particle and pore sizes for various MPNC23 batches and carbonization/graphitization temperatures ranging from 1000&nbsp;°C (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202401188-fig-0002">2a,d</a>) over 1500&nbsp;°C (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202401188-fig-0002">2b,e</a>) to 2000&nbsp;°C (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202401188-fig-0002">2c and f</a>). All prepared MPNC23 samples for this study are composed of monodisperse spherical particles that are dominantly mesoporous in nature (also see Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information). This allows to solely and independently investigate the influence of the graphitization temperature and the resulting microstructure on the electrochemical performance of such MPNC electrodes in SIB half-cells. When comparing the MPNC particles sizes of the lowest (1000&nbsp;°C) to the highest (2000&nbsp;°C) graphitization temperature, a Gaussian particle size distribution with a mean particle diameter of 289&nbsp;nm for MPNC23-1000 and 249&nbsp;nm for MPNC23-2000 (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202401188-fig-0002">2g</a>; Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information) is obtained, revealing a shrinkage of only 14 % in the chosen temperature range. The physical and structural properties of the as-prepared MPNCs were further elucidated using nitrogen physisorption, elemental analysis, Raman spectroscopy, X-ray diffraction (XRD) and scanning electron microscopy (SEM) (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202401188-fig-0002">2h–m</a>).</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202401188-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/41bc0dbd-9981-44f1-9a01-930b2fc758b6/adfm202401188-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/41bc0dbd-9981-44f1-9a01-930b2fc758b6/adfm202401188-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/7c8b260e-5868-4a08-b3a2-c1224276561a/adfm202401188-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/41bc0dbd-9981-44f1-9a01-930b2fc758b6/adfm202401188-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202401188-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202401188" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Physicochemical characterization of the prepared MPNC23 particles. SEM Micrographs of MPNC23-1000 (a,d), MPNC23-1500 (b,e), and MPNC23-2000 (c,f). Each are shown at magnifications of 10k and 150k. The samples 1000, 1300, and 1700 originate from batch A, the samples 1500 and 2000 originate from batch B and C respectively. Particle size distribution of the MPNC23-1000 and MPNC23-2000 samples as obtained by measuring 450 individual particles, showing a decrease of 14 % (g). Nitrogen adsorption and desorption isotherms recorded at 77&nbsp;K (h), Pore size distribution as acquired by QSDFT assuming slit, cylindrical and spherical pores (i). Elemental analysis showing the evolution of the atomic percentage of non-carbon atoms (mainly N, S, H, O) with increased graphitization temperatures (k), Raman spectra (l) and XRD (m) of the prepared MPNC23 temperature series and a graphite reference for comparison.<sup>[</sup><span class="figureLink bibLink tab-link"><sup><a href="#adfm202401188-bib-0080" class="figureLink bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202401188-bib-0080_R_d263571606e811" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 80">80</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The nitrogen physisorption investigations unveil type IV(a) isotherms (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202401188-fig-0002">2h</a>), exhibiting a prominently pronounced hysteresis during desorption, a characteristic commonly observed in mesoporous materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202401188-bib-0081" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202401188-bib-0081_R_d263571606e821" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 81 - Pure Appl. Chem.">81</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The isotherms are subsequently used to calculate the Brunauer–Emmett–Teller (BET) surface area (<b>Table</b> <a class="tableLink scrollableLink" title="Link to table" href="#adfm202401188-tbl-0001">1</a>). With increasing pyrolysis/graphitization temperatures, the BET&nbsp;surface area decreases from 468&nbsp;m<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> for the MPNC23-1000 sample to 174&nbsp;m<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> for the MPNC23-2000 sample.</p>
            
            <div class="article-table-content" id="adfm202401188-tbl-0001">

                    </span>Summarized physico-chemical properties of the MPNC temperature series.</header>

               </div>
               
               
               <div class="article-section__table-source"></div>
            </div>
            
            <p>The total pore volume is further obtained from these isotherms, showing a decrease from 1.26&nbsp;cm<sup>3</sup>&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> for the MPNC23-1000 sample to 0.68&nbsp;cm<sup>3</sup>&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> for the MPNC23-2000 sample, except for the MPNC23-1300 sample, which shows a slight increase with a pore volume of 1.39&nbsp;cm<sup>3</sup>&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>&nbsp;(Table&nbsp;<a class="tableLink scrollableLink" title="Link to table" href="#adfm202401188-tbl-0001">1</a>). The porosity was deconvoluted by investigating the pore size distribution, acquired by fitting the data to a quenched solid density functional theory model assuming rough surfaces in combination with cylindrical, spherical, and slit pores (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202401188-fig-0002">2i</a>). This distribution reveals most pore diameters in the samples to be between 20 and 40&nbsp;nm. From the high magnification SEM images, it is evident that the maximum intraparticular pore size present in all MPNC particles is ≈ 25 – 32&nbsp;nm, being in accordance with the chosen 23&nbsp;nm silica template. Larger pores detected (&gt;&nbsp;32&nbsp;nm) are likely caused by interparticular porosity from percolated MPNC particles in the bulk powder. To restrict the considered pore volume to intraparticular porosity, an additional pore volume for the MPNC particles is calculated by only considering the pore volume of pores between 0 and&nbsp;35&nbsp;nm (Table&nbsp;<a class="tableLink scrollableLink" title="Link to table" href="#adfm202401188-tbl-0001">1</a>). In contrast to the total pore volume, this pore volume between 0&nbsp;nm&nbsp;–&nbsp;35&nbsp;nm decreases steadily with increasing graphitization temperature, as observed for the BET surface area.</p>
            
            <p>These observations are explained by a structural collapse at higher temperatures of smaller pores (micropores) that contribute significantly to the BET surface area, causing a substantial decrease of 63 % between 1000 and 2000 °C, while the decrease in pore volume is caused by an overall decrease in the mesopore volume. This change in the material is triggered by the thermodynamically driven ongoing restructuration toward a more graphitic particle, which occurs as higher temperatures deliver sufficient energy to overcome the kinetic hindrance within the pre-linked material (compare <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202401188-fig-0003">3</a>). This restructuring also leads to a decrease in heteroatom content, mainly nitrogen and presumably oxygen (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202401188-fig-0002">2k</a>; Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information), which leave&nbsp;the structure during graphitization as gaseous species. The microstructure of the prepared carbon materials was further investigated by Raman spectroscopy (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202401188-fig-0002">2l</a>). The region between 900 and 1800&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> shows two dominant peaks, which can be further deconvoluted in contributions from four defective vibrational modes present in carbon (<i>D4</i>:&nbsp;1160 cm<sup>−1</sup>, <i>D1</i>: 1363 cm<sup>−1</sup>, <i>D3</i>:&nbsp;1533 cm<sup>−1</sup>, <i>D2</i>:&nbsp;1670&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup>) and the graphitic vibrational mode <i>G</i> at ≈1580 cm<sup>−1</sup> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information). A narrow full width at half maximum (FWHM) of this <i>G</i> band is one possible indicator for a high internal ordering.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202401188-bib-0082" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202401188-bib-0082_R_d263571606e1140" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 82 - TANSO">82</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202401188-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/8116accd-920c-4c15-8cf8-e5c24c1583d6/adfm202401188-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/8116accd-920c-4c15-8cf8-e5c24c1583d6/adfm202401188-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/62fadaf9-a3cf-48e8-a24d-99910fb6b6c4/adfm202401188-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/8116accd-920c-4c15-8cf8-e5c24c1583d6/adfm202401188-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202401188-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202401188" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">High resolution transmission electron microscopy (HRTEM) characterization of the prepared MPNC23 nanospheres. HRTEM Micrographs of MPNC23-1000 (a,d,g), of MPNC23-1500 (b,e,h) and MPNC23-2000 (c,f,i). SAED patterns of the respective samples (k,l,m) revealing the presence of three increasingly pronounced diffraction rings with increasing heat-treatment temperature, corresponding to the (002), (100), and (110) lattice planes of a more or less nanocrystalline graphitic-like carbon with a variety of orientations of the crystalline domains.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The evolution of the FWHM within the temperature series (Table&nbsp;<a class="tableLink scrollableLink" title="Link to table" href="#adfm202401188-tbl-0001">1</a>) points toward an increasing internal ordering at higher temperatures. Another strong indication of increased internal ordering is the appearance of the <i>2D1’</i> overtone at ≈2685&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> for the samples graphitized above 1500&nbsp;°C, indicating a carbon microstructure close to the inversion point of the Tuinstra-Koenig relation, with significant ordering in the a-b plane and initial ordering in the c-direction, as described by Ferrari before.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202401188-bib-0083" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202401188-bib-0083_R_d263571606e1173" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 83 - Solid State Commun.">83</a>, <a href="#adfm202401188-bib-0084" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202401188-bib-0084_R_d263571606e1173" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 84 - J. Appl. Phys.">84</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <p>The XRD patterns (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202401188-fig-0002">2m</a>) exhibit two broad reflexes, a more distinct one at ≈25° and a less dominant one between 42° and 45° corresponding to the (002) and (100/101) planes of partially crystalline carbon, respectively. It is apparent that the (002) reflex shifts toward a higher angle with increasing carbonization temperatures, indicating the narrowing of the <i>d</i><sub>002</sub> layer spacing. Using the <i>Bragg</i>-equation with <i>n</i>&nbsp;<i>λ</i> = 2&nbsp;<i>d</i>&nbsp;sin(<i>Θ</i>), the interlayer <i>d</i><sub>002</sub> spacing is readily calculated from the maximum intensity value (Table&nbsp;<a class="tableLink scrollableLink" title="Link to table" href="#adfm202401188-tbl-0001">1</a>), revealing a decrease from 3.78 to 3.45&nbsp;Å from 1000 to 2000&nbsp;°C.</p>
            
            <p>High-resolution transmission electron microscopy (HRTEM) and selected area electron diffraction (SAED) were employed to further assess the changes in microstructure and graphitization extent with increasing temperature (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202401188-fig-0003">3</a>). While the MPNC23-1000 (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202401188-fig-0003">3a,d,g,k</a>) is still a rather amorphous material, beginning with MPNC23-1500 (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202401188-fig-0003">3b,e,h,l</a>) and clearly visible for MPNC23-2000 (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202401188-fig-0003">3c,f,i,m</a>) the partially graphitic microstructure becomes evident. The graphitic domains extend ≈20&nbsp;nm in the a-b plane and up to 5&nbsp;nm in the c-direction in the case of the MPNC23-2000 sample. Further the SAED (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202401188-fig-0003">3k–m</a>) gives rise to a similar diffraction pattern as found by XRD showing the presence of &nbsp;(<i>002)</i>, (<i>100)</i>, and (<i>110) planes</i> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202401188-fig-0003">3m</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202401188-bib-0085" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202401188-bib-0085_R_d263571606e1231" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 85 - Am. Chem. Soc.">85</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The SAED analysis confirms an increasing crystallinity by revealing a much more clearly developed diffraction pattern for MPNC23-2000 when compared with MPNC23-1000. Further analysis of MPNC23-2000 reveals a <i>d</i><sub>002</sub> layer spacing of ≈0.35&nbsp;nm and a <i>d</i><sub>100</sub> layer spacing of ≈0.21&nbsp;nm (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a>, Supporting Information), matching well with the values obtained from XRD.</p>
            
            <p>The combined and coherent results from the conducted Raman, XRD, and HRTEM experiments hence clearly underline the transformation from a very low crystallinity sample (1000&nbsp;°C) toward an increasingly ordered structure, especially in the a-b plane (2000&nbsp;°C). The internal structure and ordering are expected to greatly influence the electrochemical performance in SIB half-cells, which is subsequently discussed.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202401188-sec-0040">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202401188-sec-0040-title">2.2 Electrode Fabrication</h3>
            
            <p><b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202401188-fig-0004">4</a> summarizes the process of preparing the electrodes for the electrochemical measurements. Starting with the MPNC23 powder (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202401188-fig-0004">4a</a>), an ink containing the MPNC, a Carbon Black as a conductive additive, and carboxymethyl cellulose (CMC)&nbsp;/&nbsp;styrene-butadiene rubber&nbsp;(SBR) as binder (weight ratio 8:1:0.5:0.5) was prepared (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202401188-fig-0004">4b</a>), spray coated onto aluminium foil (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202401188-fig-0004">4c</a>) and dried (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202401188-fig-0004">4d</a>). This manufacturing process resulted in electrode sheets with a wavy topography and varying thicknesses between 15 and 40&nbsp;µm within the electrode sheets (see Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202401188-fig-0004">4e</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>, Supporting Information) and a mass loading of 0.5–0.7&nbsp;mg&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup>, for an electrode with a diameter of 12&nbsp;mm (A&nbsp; = &nbsp;1.13&nbsp;cm<sup>2</sup>). A similar wavy electrode topography has been shown to facilitate high rate capabilities in LIB.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202401188-bib-0086" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202401188-bib-0086_R_d263571606e1284" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 86 - J. Mater. Chem. A">86</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> All electrochemical measurements were carried out in a three-electrode T-cell&nbsp;setup.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202401188-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/1cdb5f89-bff4-4efe-a4bc-1279d3907bd8/adfm202401188-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/1cdb5f89-bff4-4efe-a4bc-1279d3907bd8/adfm202401188-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/fba5e040-81fc-45bd-8838-6b38aba707e9/adfm202401188-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/1cdb5f89-bff4-4efe-a4bc-1279d3907bd8/adfm202401188-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202401188-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202401188" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrode manufacturing process (for more details, see Experimental Section). a) Powdery product of MPNC23 synthesis. b) Ink containing a conductive additive and a binder solution. c) Spray coating system used to coat the ink onto an aluminum sheet. d) Resulting electrode sheet after spray coating and drying at 60&nbsp;°C under vacuum. e) SEM micrograph of the electrode (more details see Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>, Supporting Information).</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
         </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202401188-sec-0050">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202401188-sec-0050-title">2.3 Electrochemical Characterization</h3>
            
            <p>The electrochemical performance was first evaluated by conducting galvanostatic discharge–charge experiments, as summarized in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202401188-fig-0005">5</a>. During the first cycle sodiation and desodiation at 40&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202401188-fig-0005">5a</a>), two distinct plateau regions are observed for all samples. The first one ≈0.5&nbsp;V versus Na/Na<sup>+</sup>&nbsp;−0.7&nbsp;V versus Na/Na<sup>+</sup> (highlighted in the corresponding colors) and a second one at ≈0.1&nbsp;V&nbsp;versus&nbsp;Na/Na<sup>+</sup> (plateau capacity), which will be discussed later. These plateaus are more defined for the samples treated at higher temperatures, with the MPNC23-1000 sample showing the most sloping profile. The first plateau can be related to the formation of a SEI layer, resulting from reductive electrolyte decomposition at the interface of the pristine carbon electrode and the electrolyte. This process is well known from LIB systems and lowers the ICE.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202401188-bib-0087" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202401188-bib-0087_R_d263571606e1338" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 87 - Sci. Rep.">87</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As this decomposition is dependent on the electrochemically active interface area, a correlation between the extent of SEI formation and the surface area of hard carbon anodes for SIB is commonly observed in literature.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202401188-bib-0053" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202401188-bib-0053_R_d263571606e1343" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 53 - Carbon">53</a>, <a href="#adfm202401188-bib-0088" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202401188-bib-0088_R_d263571606e1343" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 88 - J. Energy Chem.">88</a>, <a href="#adfm202401188-bib-0089" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202401188-bib-0089_R_d263571606e1343" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 89 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">89</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Other relevant processes in this voltage region can include sodium ion trapping on edge or defect sites,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202401188-bib-0090" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202401188-bib-0090_R_d263571606e1349" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 90 - J. Mater. Chem. A">90</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> also reducing the overall ICE. As the voltage profiles in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202401188-fig-0005">5a</a> suggest, the ICE of the prepared MPNC electrodes is comparatively low compared to most carbon materials prepared in literature,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202401188-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202401188-bib-0029_R_d263571606e1357" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Adv. Energy Mater.">29</a>, <a href="#adfm202401188-bib-0054" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202401188-bib-0054_R_d263571606e1357" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 54 - Nano Energy">54</a>, <a href="#adfm202401188-bib-0061" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202401188-bib-0061_R_d263571606e1357" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 61 - Nano Res.">61</a>, <a href="#adfm202401188-bib-0063" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202401188-bib-0063_R_d263571606e1357" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 63 - Chin. Chem. Lett.">63</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> increasing from 18 % for the MPNC23-1000 anode to 35 % for the MPNC23-2000 anode (<b>Table</b> <a class="tableLink scrollableLink" title="Link to table" href="#adfm202401188-tbl-0002">2</a>). This trend is expected, as a) the surface area and b) the heteroatom content both decrease with increasing graphitization temperatures, offering less area for the formation of a SEI and fewer surface functional groups. These functional groups can offer a wide range of energy states, resulting in a sloping voltage profile, which is strongly visible by evaluating the voltage profile of the MPNC23-1000 sample, which from all samples possesses the largest number of heteroatoms (&gt;&nbsp;12&nbsp;wt. % non-carbon atoms, compare Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202401188-fig-0002">2k</a>; Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information). In the context of a battery full cell, a low ICE proves detrimental, given its propensity to deplete the sodium ions emanating from the cathode, consequently leading to the undesirable accumulation of inactive mass and a consequent diminishment of both energy and power density. Nevertheless, this predicament could be effectively addressed by pre-sodiation of the anodes, thereby facilitating the formation of the SEI prior to the final cell assembly, as validated and exemplified in literature.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202401188-bib-0091" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202401188-bib-0091_R_d263571606e1374" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 91 - Nano Lett.">91</a>-<a href="#adfm202401188-bib-0093" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202401188-bib-0093_R_d263571606e1374" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 93 - InfoMat">93</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202401188-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/686c2c78-7d72-46c6-95fd-91c72bb4c4bb/adfm202401188-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/686c2c78-7d72-46c6-95fd-91c72bb4c4bb/adfm202401188-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/e9ae3c16-66b8-4da4-a58c-fd08ce5403a3/adfm202401188-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/686c2c78-7d72-46c6-95fd-91c72bb4c4bb/adfm202401188-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">

            </section>
